Ascent from Effect to Cause
The author enumerates three reasoning methods and applies effect-to-cause to prove God via the soul's ascent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reasoning from effect to cause (ascent from creation to Creator) leads to knowledge of God, and the role of the human soul in this ascent.

Core Idea
Effect-to-cause reasoning is the only path to know the Creator.

This passage presents a powerful argument for knowing God by ascending from effects to their ultimate cause. The human soul, being the lowest of immaterial beings, can reflect on itself and trace back through a chain of causes until it reaches the Necessary Being. This lesson breaks down the three ways of reasoning and explains why only one applies to God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ways of reasoning
Read the opening lines where the author lists three ways to move from known to unknown: cause to effect, equal to equal, and effect to cause.
Note that the first two are said to be absent regarding God.
2
Understand why only effect-to-cause applies to God
Consider why reasoning from cause to effect or equal to equal cannot be used for the Divine. The author states they are 'absent with respect to the Truth.'
God has no equal and is not an effect of a higher cause.
3
Trace the soul's ascent through intermediaries
The human soul is the lowest immaterial being. From knowledge of itself, it ascends to knowledge of its cause, then to the cause of that cause, until reaching the Necessary Being.
This ascent is gradual, not immediate.
4
Reflect on the Quranic support
The author cites verses that describe God as the ultimate end and the Last, reinforcing the idea of return to Him.
Verses: 53:42, 42:53, 57:3.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة لهم في هذا الباب إن الانتقال من المعلوم إلى المجهول لا يعقل إلا بأحد ثلاثة أوجه: أحدها: الاستدلال بالعلة على المعلول. وثانيها: الاستدلال بالمساوى على المساوى. وثالثها: الاستدلال بالمعلول على العلة. والطريقان الأولان في حق الحق مفقودان فبقي الطريق الثالث وهو أن يصعد من الأثر إلى المؤثر، وينتقل من المخلوق إلى الخالق. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: النفس الناطقة الإنسانية واقعة في المرتبة الآخرة من الموجودات المجردة المقدسة، على ما ستعرف حقيقة هذه المقدمة عند وقوفك على معرفة درجات الملائكة ومراتبها، وإذا كان كذلك فهذه النفس الإنسانية تترقى من علمها بنفسها إلى علمها بعلتها، ومن علمها بعلتها إلى علمها بعلة علتها، وهكذا تترقى مرتبة فمرتبة حتى تصل بالآخرة إلى حضرة واجب الوجود لذاته. كما قال في الكتاب الإلهي: "وأن إلى ربك المنتهى" وقال: "ألا إلى الله تصير الأمور" وقال: "هو الأول والآخر" فالحق هو الأول عند النزول من الحق إلى الخلق، والآخر عند الصعود من الخلق إلى الحق ولما كانت درجات الوسائط
The fourth argument for them in this chapter: that transition from the known to the unknown is only conceivable by one of three ways: first, reasoning from cause to effect; second, reasoning from equal to equal; third, reasoning from effect to cause. The first two ways are absent with respect to the Truth, so the third way remains, which is to ascend from the effect to the agent, and to transition from the creature to the Creator. And if you know this, we say: the human rational soul occupies the lowest rank among the abstract holy existents, as you will know the reality of this premise when you come to know the degrees of angels and their ranks. Since this is so, this human soul ascends from its knowledge of itself to its knowledge of its cause, and from its knowledge of its cause to its knowledge of the cause of its cause, and so on ascending rank by rank until it ultimately reaches the presence of the Necessary Being in itself. As it is said in the Divine Book: 'And that to your Lord is the final end' (53:42), and: 'Surely to God all matters return' (42:53), and: 'He is the First and the Last' (57:3). So the Truth is the First in descent from the Truth to creation, and the Last in ascent from creation to the Truth. And since the degrees of intermediaries...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument of the opponents (or a group) regarding the human intellect's ability to know God. It begins by stating that transition from known to unknown is possible only via three ways: cause to effect, equal to equal, and effect to cause. The first two are inapplicable to God, leaving only the third: ascent from effect to cause. The author then specifies that the human rational soul is the lowest of the immaterial beings (a premise to be clarified later from angelology). This soul ascends from knowledge of itself to knowledge of its cause, then to the cause of that cause, continuing until it reaches the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود). Quranic verses are cited: 'And that to your Lord is the final end' (53:42), and others. The passage ends with the statement that God is the First in descent (from Him to creation) and the Last in ascent (from creation to Him). The argument shows a gradual metaphysical ascent through intermediaries.

نفس ناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think reasoning from effect to cause is immediate, giving direct knowledge of God without intermediate causes.
Correction: The passage stresses that the ascent proceeds gradually through intermediate causes ('rank by rank'), not instantly. The soul first knows its immediate cause, then the cause of that cause, and so on until the Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
Which of the three ways of reasoning is applied to know God according to this passage?
A) Reasoning from cause to effect.
B) Reasoning from equal to equal.
C) Reasoning from effect to cause.
D) Reasoning from a first principle.

Key Takeaway
The only viable path to know God through reason is to ascend from effects (like the human soul) through intermediate causes to the ultimate Cause, the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own soul, do you truly ascend from self-awareness to awareness of your Creator, or do you remain trapped in self-contemplation? What is one step you can take today to move from knowledge of yourself to knowledge of your Cause?
?
The passage says the soul ascends 'rank by rank' through intermediaries. Are you actively seeking to understand the causes and means that lead to God, or do you expect an immediate leap without effort? Name one intermediate cause you can study this week.
?
You have learned that the only path to know God is through effect-to-cause reasoning. Examine your daily thoughts: how often do you reason from the created things you see back to the Creator? Starting right now, look at the object in front of you and mentally trace it back to its ultimate Cause. Does your heart sense the connection?

Third Knot: The Problem of Attributing Effects to Causes
Author introduces a problem about attributing effects to causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribution of a specific effect to a specific cause is a profound theological and philosophical puzzle that challenges assumptions of necessary causality.

Core Idea
The connection between a cause and its effect is not rationally necessary.

The author introduces the third knot, which concerns knowledge of divine actions. He points out that our intellects are bewildered when trying to understand how a specific effect can be attributed to a specific agent. This passage sets up a deep discussion about causality and divine agency, questioning whether such attribution is self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording convey the sense of bewilderment.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the difficulty described
The author says there is a station where intellects are bewildered. What is that station? It is the question of how a specific effect is attributed to a specific cause. Note that he says it is 'not determined at all' (لا يتعين البتة).
3
Reflect on why the author calls it a station of bewilderment
Think about why this attribution is not obvious. In everyday experience, we assume causes produce effects necessarily. The author suggests this connection is not rationally determined, which is puzzling.
This challenges common sense.
4
Consider the implication for causality
If attribution is not determined, then how do we know which cause produces which effect? This opens the door to discussion of divine agency and occasionalism.
The full argument likely continues in subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معرفة الأفعال : ففيه موقف حارت فيه العقول وضلت الأفهام، وهو أن إسناد الأثر المعين إلى مؤثر لا يتعين البتة كيف يعقل؟
As for knowledge of actions: in it there is a station at which intellects are bewildered and understandings are lost, and it is: how is it comprehensible that the attribution of a specific effect to an agent is not determined at all?
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage introduces the third knot: knowledge of divine actions. It states that there is a station (موقف) regarding this topic that confuses intellects and misguides understandings. The problem is: how can one rationally attribute a specific effect to a specific cause? The author implies that the connection is not self-evident or necessary; it is not determined (لا يتعين). This sets up a discussion on causality and divine agency, but the text in this window cuts off. The author likely continues to explain the difficulty and perhaps a solution.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing an effect to a cause is obvious and self-evident.
Correction: The author argues that such attribution is not determined and it bewilders intellects, implying it is a deep philosophical puzzle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens to intellects when considering the attribution of a specific effect to an agent?
A) They are illuminated
B) They are bewildered
C) They find clear proof
D) They are reassured

Key Takeaway
The attribution of a specific effect to a specific cause is not rationally determined, and this puzzles the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day and expect certain results from your actions, do you ever stop to question whether those cause-effect links are truly necessary? How does it feel to realize they might not be determined at all?
?
Have you ever been deeply confused by an event that seemed to have no rational cause? How does the author's description of 'intellects bewildered and understandings lost' match your own experience?
?
If the connection between your efforts and their outcomes is not rationally determined, what does that do to your sense of control? Are you willing to live with that uncertainty, or do you try to explain it away?

The Problem of Attributing Effects to an Unchanging Cause
The author presents the central dilemma of divine action and critiques two incomplete solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core dilemma in attributing specific effects to a cause that is not itself subject to change, and why both temporal origination and eternal existence views face this difficulty.

Core Idea
How can an unchanging cause produce a specific effect at a specific time?

In this passage, the author presents a profound puzzle that has perplexed theologians and philosophers: how can a specific effect be attributed to a cause that is not itself determined to act? Without a change in the cause—such as a new will, a change in time, or removal of an obstacle—it seems impossible for the cause to become an agent. This dilemma has significant implications for understanding how God, who is unchanging, can create a temporal world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the central question
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the first sentence that states the bewilderment: how can a specific effect be attributed to a non-determined cause?
The passage begins with 'وأما معرفة الأفعال'.
2
Examine the conditions for agency
Notice the conditions listed: will, change of time, occurrence of benefit, removal of obstacle. Why are these necessary for a cause to become an agent?
These conditions apply to created agents, not necessarily to God.
3
Analyze the two groups' positions
The author mentions two groups: those who affirm the world's temporal origination and those who affirm its eternity. What does each group think they achieve?
The huduth group must solve the problem; the qidam group thought they escaped it.
4
Understand why the problem persists
Even with an eternal world, temporal changes occur. If every change depends on another change, infinite regress follows. If all changes depend on an unchanging being, the original problem returns.
The passage ends without resolution, setting up future discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معرفة الأفعال : ففيه موقف حارت فيه العقول وضلت الأفهام، وهو أن إسناد الأثر المعين إلى مؤثر لا يتعين البتة كيف يعقل؟ فإنه ما لم يحدث له إرادة أو تغير وقت أو حدوث مصلحة أو زوال عائق فإنه يمتنع أن يصير فاعلا بعد أن لم يكن كذلك. وأما القائلون بحدوث العالم : فقد احتاجوا إلى دفع هذه العقدة. وأما القائلون بقدم العالم : فقد ظنوا أنهم تخلصوا من هذه العقدة، وليس الأمر كذلك، فإنه لا شك في حدوث الصور والأعراض في هذا العالم وأن هذه الأحوال قد توجد بعد عدمها وتعدم بعد وجودها فإن أسندنا كل حادث إلى حادث آخر من غير استنادها إلى موجود قديم فهو محال، وإن وجب انتهاؤها واستنادها بالآخرة إلى موجود هو واجب الوجود لذاته، منزه عن جهات التغير، فقد عاد الإشكال.
As for knowledge of actions: it involves a position at which minds are bewildered and understandings are led astray, namely: how can it be rationally conceived that a specific effect is attributed to a cause that is not specifically determined? For as long as there has not occurred for it a will, or a change of time, or the occurrence of a benefit, or the removal of an obstacle, then it is impossible for it to become an agent after not having been so. As for those who affirm the temporal origination of the world: they have needed to untie this knot. As for those who affirm the eternity of the world: they thought they had escaped from this knot, but it is not so. For there is no doubt about the temporal origination of forms and accidents in this world, and that these states come to be after their non-existence and cease after their existence. If we ascribe every temporal event to another temporal event without its ultimately depending on an eternal existent, that is impossible. And if it must terminate and depend ultimately on an existent that is necessary by itself, free from aspects of change, then the problem returns.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage addresses the problem of divine action (afi‘al). The author states that minds are bewildered by how a specific effect is attributed to a cause that is not specifically determined. In human experience, one only becomes an agent through a cause like will, a change in time, etc. Without such a change, it is impossible to become an agent after not being one. Then the author examines two groups: those who affirm the world's temporal origination (huduth) must solve this knot; those who affirm its eternity (qidam) thought they escaped it but did not. Because even if the world is eternal, forms and accidents constantly change—they come and go. If every new event is explained by another temporal event without an eternal foundation, that is infinite regress (impossible). If all events must ultimately depend on a self-necessary being free from change, then the problem returns: how does this unchanging being cause change? Thus the difficulty remains unresolved in this passage.

Key Terms
أثر
مؤثر
إرادة
حدوث العالم
قدم العالم
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage concludes that the problem is unsolvable.
Correction: The passage presents the problem as a difficulty that must be resolved; it does not claim insolubility, but leaves it unresolved for now.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did proponents of the world's eternity mistakenly think they had escaped?
A) The problem of attributing a specific effect to an unchanging cause
B) The problem of the world's temporal events
C) The problem of predestination
D) The problem of evil

Key Takeaway
The problem of how an unchanging cause generates temporal effects is a fundamental dilemma in theology, affecting both those who affirm and deny the world's temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in an unchanging God, yet you pray for specific needs at specific times. Do you ever feel a tension between God's immutability and your own experiences of change? How do you reconcile this in your heart when your prayers seem unanswered?
?
This passage reveals a deep intellectual knot that even great theologians have struggled with. When you encounter such a mystery in your faith, do you tend to ignore it, rationalize it, or let it deepen your reliance on God? Which path does your soul actually take?
?
If you truly accepted that God's action cannot be explained by our categories of cause and effect, how would that change the way you approach your daily life—your plans, your anxieties, your hopes? Would you live with more trust or more uncertainty?

Is Causality Self-Evident? Evidence from Instinct
The author argues that the need for a cause is self-evident, using examples from adults, children, and animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the principle that every contingent thing requires a cause is considered self-evident by many rational thinkers, based on instinctive human and animal behavior.

Core Idea
The need for a cause is instinctive, not learned.

This lesson introduces a foundational debate in Islamic theology: is the principle of causality self-evident or demonstrative? The author presents the view that it is self-evident, supported by observations of human and animal behavior. As you read, notice how the argument moves from rational adults to children to even beasts, showing the universality of this instinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage uses concrete examples to make an abstract point.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage starts by stating two positions: one holds the premise to be self-evident (badihi), the other holds it to be demonstrative (burhani). Note which side the author defends.
The rest of the passage is evidence for the self-evident view.
3
Analyze the three levels of evidence
The author gives three sets of examples: rational adults reacting to events, children reacting to missing items, and beasts fleeing from danger. For each, ask: how does this show instinctive causality?
The argument moves from the most rational to the least rational beings, showing universality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that this knowledge is the strongest of self-evident sciences. Consider why he thinks it is even stronger than ordinary self-evident truths.
If you find this convincing, you side with the 'badihi' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن العقلاء لهم في هذا الموقف قولان : الأول : أن هذه المقدمة بديهية والثاني : أنها برهانية . أما القائلون بالقول الأول فقد احتجوا على صحة مذهبهم بأن قالوا : إنـا رأينا جمهور العقلاء مطبقين على أنهم إذا أحسوا بحدوث حادث طلبوا له سبباً ، وإذا سمعوا صوت إنسان اضطروا إلى العلم بحضور ذلك الإنسان ، وإذا رأوا حدوث بناء قطعوا بوجود باني ، بل نزيد ونقول : إن هذا العلم حاصل في نفوس الأطفال الذين لم يبلغوا إلى كمال العقل ، وذلك لأن الطفل إذا كان له مكان وموضع يختص هو به بالتصرف فيه ، فإذا وجد فيه طعاماً لم يضعه فيه ، أو غاب عنه شيء وضعه فيه ، فإنه يصيح ، ويقول : من الذي أخذه ؟ ومن الذي وضعه ؟ وذلك يدل على أن فطرة ذلك الطفل تشهد بأن الممكن لا بد له من مرجح ، والحادث لا بد له من محدث . وإذا كان هذا العلم مركوزاً في غريزة نفس ذلك الطفل ، علمنا أنه أقوى العلوم البديهية . بل نقول : إن هذا النوع من الإدراك مركوز في نفوس البهائم ، وذلك لأن البهيمة إذا سمعت صوت الحية ، فرت . وليس فرارها إلا لأن شعـورهـا بصوت الحية يقتضي شعورها بوجود الحية ، فدل هذا على أن انتقال الفطرة والنفس من الأثر إلى المؤثر حاصل في نفوس الأطفال بل هو أمر حاصل في نفوس البهائم
Know that the rational people have two positions on this stance: the first is that this premise is self-evident, and the second is that it is demonstrative. As for those who hold the first position, they have argued for the correctness of their doctrine by saying: We see the majority of rational people unanimously that when they perceive the occurrence of an event they seek a cause for it, and when they hear a human voice they are compelled to knowledge of the presence of that human, and when they see the occurrence of a building they decisively judge the existence of a builder. Rather, we add and say: This knowledge is present in the souls of children who have not attained complete reason. That is because when a child has a place and location that he specifically has the right to dispose of, and he finds there food that he did not put there, or something of his disappears and (someone) placed something there, he screams and says: 'Who took it? Who placed it?' That indicates that the innate disposition (fitra) of that child testifies that the contingent (mumkin) necessarily has a preponderator (murajjih), and the originated (hadith) necessarily has an originator (muhdith). And since this knowledge is ingrained in the instinct (ghariza) of that child's soul, we know that it is the strongest of self-evident sciences. Rather, we say: This type of perception is ingrained in the souls of beasts. That is because when a beast hears the sound of a snake, it flees. Its fleeing is only because its perception of the snake's sound necessitates its perception of the snake's existence. So this indicates that the transfer from effect to cause in innate disposition and soul is present in the souls of children; rather, it is present in the souls of beasts.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents an argument defending the view that the principle 'every contingent thing requires a cause' (or more precisely, 'every possible event requires a preponderator') is self-evident (badihi) rather than known through demonstration (burhani). The author begins by stating that there are two positions: some hold the premise to be self-evident, others hold it to be demonstrative. The proponents of the self-evident position offer several pieces of evidence. First, they observe that the majority of rational people, upon perceiving an event (hadith), instinctively seek a cause for it; upon hearing a human voice, they are compelled to know of the presence of the speaker; upon seeing the construction of a building, they immediately judge that a builder exists. Second, they argue that this knowledge is present even in children who have not reached full intellectual maturity. The author illustrates with an example: a child who has a private place finds food or missing objects that he did not put there, and he instinctively asks 'Who took it?' and 'Who placed it?' This shows that the child's innate disposition (fitra) testifies that a contingent thing (mumkin) must have a preponderator (murajjih) and an originated thing (hadith) must have an originator (muhdith). Since this knowledge is ingrained in the child's instinct (ghariza), it is even stronger than ordinary self-evident truths. Third, the author extends this argument to beasts: when an animal hears the sound of a snake, it flees, and its flight indicates a perception that the sound implies the snake's existence. This demonstrates that the instinctive transition from effect to cause is present not only in children but even in beasts. Thus, the author concludes that this knowledge is the most powerful of self-evident truths.

Key Terms
بديهية
برهانية
مرجح
محدث
ممكن
فطرة
غريزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the need for a cause is only known through philosophical demonstration.
Correction: The author argues it is instinctive and universally recognized, even by children and animals.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence do proponents of the self-evident view use to support their position?
A) They argue that children and animals instinctively infer causes from effects.
B) They claim that the principle is proven by logical syllogisms.
C) They rely on scriptural revelation.
D) They say that everyone agrees after philosophical training.

Key Takeaway
The principle of causality is so fundamental that even children and animals instinctively recognize it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that even a child instinctively asks 'Who took it?' when something disappears. When you experience a blessing, do you instinctively ask 'Who gave it?' or do you take it for granted? What would it take for you to return to that childlike instinct, and are you willing to do that today?
?
The author says the knowledge of causality is ingrained even in beasts. You are not a beast—you are a rational being. Yet when you see the signs of creation, do you instinctively turn to the Creator, or have you rationalized it away? What excuse will you offer on the Day when every soul will be questioned about its fitra?
?
If the instinct of causality is so strong that a child screams for a cause, why do you sometimes suppress that instinct when it comes to the ultimate cause? Are you afraid of what it demands? Commit today to let your fitra speak without interference, and act on what it tells you about the Creator.

Objection and Answer: Contingency vs. Origination
The author refutes the objection that the examples only prove need for a cause for originated things, not for contingent things as such.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the need for a cause is self-evident only for originated things, not for contingent things as such.

Core Idea
Self-evident causality is about change, not mere possibility.

After establishing the original argument about self-evident causality, the author addresses a critical objection. The objector claims that the examples only show that originated events need a cause, not that contingent things in general do. The author's response reveals a deeper philosophical point: our certainty about needing a cause is tied to the idea of origination (huduth), not to pure contingency (imkan). This lesson unpacks that objection and the subtle answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note how the objector distinguishes between 'originated' (muhdath) and 'contingent' (mumkin).
The objection claims that self-evident knowledge only applies to originated things.
2
Understand the counter-argument
The objector anticipates a counter: we know self-evidently that when existence and non-existence are equal, a preponderator is needed. This would apply to all contingent things.
This counter seems to bridge the gap between origination and contingency.
3
Follow the author's analysis
The author responds by analyzing why the intellect judges that a preponderator is needed. He says it is because we imagine the preponderance as an occurrence (huduth).
The certainty comes from the idea of change, not from abstract possibility.
4
Consider the proof: the enduring contingent
The author asks us to remove all thought of origination and consider a contingent thing that already exists and endures (al-mumkin al-baqi). In that state, we do not have a necessary judgment that it needs a cause.
This thought experiment shows that our intuition is tied to newness, not mere contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل الكلام على ما ذكرتم من وجوه : الأول : إن هذه الاعتبارات التي ذكرتم إن صحت ، فإنها إنما تدل على أن العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر علم ضروري ولكنها لا تدل على أن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر علم ضروري، فأين أحد البابين عن الآخر ؟ فإن قال قائل : إنا نجد العلم البديهي حاصلاً بأن الوجود والعدم لما استويا استحال رجحان أحدهما على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، فعلمنا أن العلم البديهي كما حصل في افتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر ، فهو أيضاً حاصل في افتقار الممكن إلى المرجح . فنقول في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : إن قولكم الوجود والعدم لما استويا بالنسبة إليه لم يترجح أحدهما على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، إنما جزم العقل به ، لأن أحد الطرفين على الآخر يوهم حدوث ذلك الرجحان ، قولكم يترجح وإنه أمر حصل بعد أن لم يكن . فهذا الجزم إنما حصل من تخيل معنى الحدوث لا من نفس الإمكان المجرد ، والدليل عليه : أنا إذا أزلنا وهم الحدوث بالكلية ، واعتبرنا معنى الإمكان خاليا عن توهم الحدوث ، وذلك في الممكن الباقي في حال بقائه لا نجد في العقل جزماً بافتقار الممكن حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ، بل الغالب على الأوهام استغناء الباقي في حال بقائه عن المؤثر
If it is said: Discussion on what you mentioned has aspects: First, these observations you mentioned, if they are sound, only indicate that knowledge of the need of the originated (muhdath) for an effecter (muʼaththir) is necessary (daruri) knowledge, but they do not indicate that knowledge of the need of the contingent (mumkin) for an effecter is necessary knowledge. So how far is one door from the other? If someone says: We find self-evident knowledge that existence and non-existence, when they are equal (with respect to it), it is impossible for one to be preponderant over the other except by a preponderator (murajjih). So we know that self-evident knowledge, just as it occurs regarding the need of the originated for an effecter, also occurs regarding the need of the contingent for a preponderator. Then we say in answer to this question: Your statement 'existence and non-existence, when they are equal with respect to it, one does not become preponderant over the other except by a preponderator' — the intellect only decisively judges it because one of the two sides over the other suggests the occurrence (huduth) of that preponderance. Your statement 'it becomes preponderant' and that it is something that occurs after having not been — this decisive judgment only arises from imagining the meaning of origination (huduth), not from pure contingency (imkan) itself. The proof of this is: If we remove the notion of origination entirely and consider the meaning of contingency devoid of imagining origination — and that is in the contingent enduring (al-mumkin al-baqi) in its state of endurance — we do not find in the intellect a decisive judgment about the need of the contingent in its state of endurance for an effecter. Rather, the prevalent in imaginations is the independence of the enduring (al-baqi) in its state of endurance from an effecter.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The author presents an objection to the earlier argument that the need for a cause is self-evident. The objection makes a crucial distinction: the examples given (children, animals, etc.) only prove that people instinctively know that an originated event (muhdath) requires a cause (muʼaththir). They do not prove that a contingent thing (mumkin) as such — without reference to origination — requires a cause. The objector thus claims that self-evident knowledge applies only to originated things, not to contingent things in general. The author then anticipates a potential counter-argument: someone might say that self-evident knowledge also applies to contingency because we know that if a thing's existence and non-existence are equally possible, one cannot become actual without a preponderator. The author replies to this counter-argument by analyzing the reasoning. He argues that when we say 'one side becomes preponderant over the other only by a preponderator,' the intellect's certainty is based on the idea that preponderance is an occurrence (huduth) that happens after not being. Thus, the certainty arises from imagining the concept of origination (huduth), not from the abstract concept of contingency (imkan). To prove this, the author considers a contingent thing that endures (al-mumkin al-baqi) — i.e., something that is contingent in itself but already exists and continues to exist. In that case, if we remove all thought of origination, we do not find in the intellect any necessary judgment that it needs a cause in its state of endurance. In fact, common imaginations tend to assume that something enduring does not need a cause while it endures. Therefore, the self-evident necessity applies only to origination, not to contingency in the abstract. This answer defends the position that the need for a cause is not self-evident for all contingent things, only for originated ones, and thus the original evidence remains valid for the latter.

Correction: The passage shows that the intuitive certainty is tied to origination (huduth), not to contingency (imkan) in the abstract. For a contingent thing that endures, we do not have the same certainty.

D) The testimony of scripture.

Key Takeaway
The self-evident necessity of a cause applies to originated events, not to contingent things in general, because the certainty stems from the notion of change (huduth).

Innate Inference from Effect to Cause in Animals
Author argues instinctive causality in animals using a behavioral example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the ability to infer a cause from its effect is not merely rational but is ingrained in the natural disposition (fitrah) of all living beings, including animals.

Core Idea
The inference from effect to cause is instinctive, not just rational.

This lesson presents a compelling argument that the knowledge of causality is not acquired solely through reasoning but is deeply embedded in our natural disposition. The author uses the example of a beast reacting to a snake's sound to illustrate that even animals instinctively perceive the cause from the effect. This insight grounds the proof for a creator in a universal, innate principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the author’s claim and the example given.
The passage is concise; each word matters.
2
Identify the claim and evidence
Find the main claim: the perception of cause from effect is ingrained in beasts. Then identify the example (snake's sound → fleeing) and how it proves the claim.
3
Connect to fitrah
Reflect on why the author calls this a 'transition of the fitrah'. How does this example show that causality is innate?
Fitrah refers to the original natural state of creation.
4
Consider the broader implication
Think about how this argument strengthens the case for the existence of a creator. If even animals instinctively infer cause from effect, then belief in a cause for the universe is natural.
This is a key point in Islamic theology.

Commentary
This passage is an argument by the author to demonstrate that the knowledge of the necessity of a cause for an effect is not just a rational deduction but is ingrained in the natural disposition (fitrah) of all living beings, including animals. The author uses the example of a beast fleeing upon hearing the sound of a snake. The beast does not see the snake but hears its sound, yet it immediately perceives the existence of the snake and flees. This shows that the transition from the effect (the sound) to the cause (the snake) is a natural, instinctive process, not a learned rational inference. The author then extends this to children, indicating that this is innate in humans as well, and even in animals.

The Basis of Causal Necessity: Occurrence vs. Possibility
Objection and answer on the scope of intuitive causal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need for a cause is not known necessarily for mere possibility, but only when the notion of occurrence is involved.

Core Idea
The mind only judges a thing needs a cause when it imagines it coming into being.

This passage tackles a crucial objection: is the need for a cause known by intuition for all possible existences, or only for originated ones? The author argues that our intuitive certainty actually stems from the idea of occurrence, not from possibility alone. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objector's claim: that the need for a cause is only known intuitively for originated things, not for all possible things. Note the distinction between 'muhdath' (originated) and 'mumkin' (possible).
The objector anticipates a reply and rejects it.
2
Identify the objector's reasoning and anticipated reply
The objector says the principle 'when existence and non-existence are equal, a preponderator is needed' is only intuitive for originated things. He then entertains a possible counter-argument that it also applies to possible things, but he dismisses it.
Pay attention to the key terms 'مرجح' (preponderator) and 'حدوث' (occurrence).
3
Read the author's response
The author responds by analyzing the basis of the intuitive judgment. He argues that the mind only asserts the need for a preponderator because it imagines the preponderance as an occurrence—something that came to be. He then provides evidence by considering a possible thing in its state of persistence.
The author uses the example of a persisting possible thing to show that we do not intuitively judge it needs a cause.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
The author concludes that the necessity of a cause is tied to the notion of occurrence (huduth), not to mere possibility (imkan). Reflect on how this distinction affects the overall argument for a Creator.
This is a subtle but important point in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For if it is said: The discourse is upon what you mentioned in several aspects: First: These considerations that you mentioned, if they are correct, only indicate that the knowledge of the originated's need for an agent is necessary knowledge, but they do not indicate that the knowledge of the possible's need for an agent is necessary knowledge. So how far apart are the two categories? Then if someone says: We find that intuitive knowledge occurs that when existence and non-existence are equal, the preponderance of one over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. So we know that intuitive knowledge, just as it occurs regarding the originated's need for an agent, also occurs regarding the possible's need for a preponderator. Then we say in answer to this question: Your statement that when existence and non-existence are equal in relation to something, one does not become preponderant over the other except by a preponderator, the mind only asserts this because one of the two sides over the other implies the occurrence of that preponderance. Your statement that it becomes preponderant and that it is a thing that came to be after it was not. So this assertion only arises from imagining the meaning of occurrence (al-huduth), not from mere abstract possibility (al-imkan al-mujarrad). The evidence for it is that if we remove the imagination of occurrence entirely, and consider the meaning of possibility free from the notion of occurrence, as in the case of a possible thing that persists in its state of persistence, we find no assertion in the mind of the possible's need, in its state of persistence, for an agent. Rather, the prevailing impression is that the persisting thing, in its state of persistence, is independent of an agent, and that it persists by itself until the time of the arrival of the remover or annihilator. So we know that this assertion is only obtained at the occurrence of origination, or at the imagination of the meaning of occurrence. As for possibility when stripped of the imagination of occurrence, the clear intellect does not judge it to be a cause for its need of the other.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the author's position and the author's response. The objector argues that the intuitive knowledge of the need for a cause applies only to things that are originated (al-muhdath) — things that come into existence after non-existence — but does not necessarily apply to possible existences (al-mumkin) in general. He claims that the principle "when existence and non-existence are equal, a preponderator is needed" is only known intuitively for originated things, not for all possible things. The objector then anticipates a reply and rejects it. The author responds by analyzing the basis of the intuitive judgment. He argues that when we say that one side must have a preponderator, the mind is actually considering the occurrence of the preponderance — the fact that it came to be after not being. Thus, the judgment is based on the notion of occurrence (huduth), not on bare possibility (imkan). To prove this, the author asks us to consider a possible thing that continues to exist — in its state of persistence, we do not intuitively judge that it needs a cause. In fact, the common assumption is that a persisting thing is independent of a cause until something removes it. Therefore, the necessity of a cause arises only when we imagine occurrence, not from possibility alone.

Correction: The author shows that the intuitive judgment is actually tied to the notion of occurrence (huduth), not to mere possibility (imkan). For a persisting possible thing, we do not intuitively judge it needs a cause.

Second Objection: Three Arguments Against a Necessary Cause
The author presents a second objection with three sub-arguments challenging the necessity of a cause for contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments presented as the second objection against the necessity of a cause for contingent things, and that these are objections the author will later refute.

Core Idea
The necessity of a cause is contested by common agreement, human choice, and essence equality.

The author now presents a second set of objections that challenge the core kalam principle that every contingent needs a cause. These objections are not the author's own view but are drawn from various schools of thought. They will be refuted later. This passage gives three distinct arguments that appeal to common agreement, everyday examples, and metaphysical equality of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without overanalyzing. Get a sense of the three arguments being presented.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage has three numbered sections: الأول, الثاني, الثالث. Find the core claim of each argument. Notice how each one tries to show that something occurs without a cause.
The first argument appeals to majority opinion, the second to human choice, the third to essence equality.
3
Understand the objection's purpose
Remember that this is an objection against the kalam position. The author does not endorse these arguments; he is presenting them to later refute. Consider how strong each argument seems on its own.
The common misunderstanding is that these are the author's own views.
4
Reflect on the variety of examples
Notice the different types of evidence: majority consensus, everyday experience (choice between equal options), and metaphysical reasoning (essence division). This shows the breadth of the challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني على أصل الكلام : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن العقلاء يجزمون بافتقار الممكن إلى المرجح ، والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن القائلين بحدوث العالم أكثر عدداً من القائلين بقدمه ، ثم إنهم مع كثرتهم يلتزمون أنه تعالى صار فاعلاً للعالم بعد أن لم يكن فاعلاً له ثم اتفقوا على أن تجدد هذه الفاعلية ليس بسبب ، فهؤلاء قد اتفقوا على حصول معنى الحدوث والتجدد في هذه الصورة لا لسبب ، ولو كان امتناع ذلك معلوما بالضرورة لامتنع إطباق العقلاء عليه . الثاني : إنهم يقولون : الهارب من السبع إذا عن له طريقان متساويان من كل الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني ، لا لمرجح ، وكذلك من خير بين الشرب من قدحين من الماء متساويين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني لا لمرجح وفي أمثلة هذا الباب كثرة مشهورة. فههنا اتفق الأكثرون على أنه يحصل الرجحان لا المرجح ، ولو كانت هذه المقدمة معلومة الامتناع بالبديهية لما ذهب إلى القول بصحتها طائفة عظيمة من العقلاء . الثالث : إن طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين ذهبوا إلى أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات قالوا ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن الذات يمكن تقسيمها إلى الواجب والممكن والمجرد والمادي فمورد التقسيم مشترك بين الأقسام .
The second objection to the original claim: We say: We do not concede that intelligent people are certain of the possible's need for a preponderator. Evidence for this is several aspects: First: Those who assert the creation of the world are more numerous than those who assert its eternity. Yet they, despite their number, commit to the idea that He, exalted, became an agent of the world after not being an agent of it, and then they agree that this renewal of agency is without a cause. So they all agree on the occurrence of the meaning of origination and renewal in this instance without a cause. If the impossibility of that were known by necessity, it would be impossible for the intelligent to agree upon it. Second: They say: The person fleeing from a beast, if he faces two equally balanced paths in every respect, chooses one over the other without a preponderator. Likewise, one who is given the choice between drinking from two equal cups of water chooses one over the other without a preponderator. There are many well-known examples of this type. So here the majority agree that preponderance occurs without a preponderator. If this premise were known to be impossible by intuition, a great number of intelligent people would not have held it to be valid. Third: A great group of mutakallimun held that essences are equal in being essences. They said, and it is indicated by two aspects: First, the essence can be divided into the necessary, the possible, the abstract, and the material, so the subject of division is common among the divisions.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection against the necessity of a cause for contingent things, consisting of three distinct arguments. The objector denies that rational people universally accept the principle that every possible contingent needs a preponderator. The first argument notes that most people believe the world is created, yet they hold that God became an agent of the world after not being an agent, and they agree that this change occurred without any cause. If it were intuitively impossible for something to occur without a cause, the majority would not hold such a belief. The second argument uses examples from human choice: a person fleeing from a predator, when faced with two equal paths, chooses one without a cause; similarly, someone choosing between two equal cups of water does so without a cause. Many such examples are well-known. The majority of rational people affirm preponderance without a preponderator in these cases, which contradicts the claimed intuitive necessity. The third argument refers to a group of mutakallimun who hold that essences are inherently equal; they argue that because essence can be divided into different categories (necessary, possible, abstract, material), the basis of division (essence itself) must be common, implying equality. This challenges the distinctness that might necessitate different causal dependencies.

Key Terms
ممكن (possible)
مرجح (preponderator)
ذوات (essences)
متساوية (equal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These three arguments represent the author's own view on causality.
Correction: The author is presenting an objection that he will later refute. These arguments are not his position but challenges from other schools.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument in the second objection?
A) The majority of people believe the world is eternal
B) Many people say God became an agent after not being one without a cause
C) A person chooses between two equal paths without a cause
D) The essences of things are equal

Key Takeaway
The second objection challenges the necessity of a cause by appealing to common belief, human choice, and essential equality.

Only Temporal Origination Renders Contingency Necessitating a Cause
Concludes that certainty of need for a cause requires temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intellect's judgment that a contingent thing needs a cause depends on temporal origination (huduth) rather than on mere possibility (imkan).

Core Idea
Mere possibility does not make the intellect see a need for a cause; only temporal origination does.

This passage concludes a crucial point in the kalam argument for the existence of Allah. The author argues that the certainty that a contingent being requires a cause is not based on its possibility alone, but on its temporal origination or the imagination thereof. This distinction is vital for understanding why the kalam cosmological argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the source passage once to get the flow. Note the key terms: الإمكان, الحدوث, الغير.
2
Identify the two scenarios contrasted
The passage contrasts two cases: when temporal origination is present or imagined, and when possibility is stripped of any notion of origination. Notice the different verdicts of the intellect.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If possibility alone sufficed, why would the author insist on tying it to huduth? This helps clarify the kalam position on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه يبقى بذاته إلى وقت طريان المزيل المعدم، فعلمنا أن ذلك الجزم لا يحصل إلا عند حصول الحدوث، أو عند تخيل معنى الحدوث، وأما الإمكان إذا عرى عن خيال الحدوث فإن صريح العقل لا يحكم بكونه سبباً لاحتياجه إلى الغير.
And that it remains with its essence until the time when the remover and annihilator occurs. So we know that that certainty does not occur except when temporal origination occurs, or when imagining the meaning of temporal origination. As for possibility when it is stripped of the imagination of temporal origination, then the clear intellect does not judge that it is a cause for its need for the other.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage concludes a preceding argument. It states that the certainty that something needs a cause (al-ghayr) only comes about when we have actual temporal origination (huduth) or when we imagine it. In contrast, pure possibility (imkan) without any association with temporal origination does not lead the intellect to judge that it requires a cause. The author is defending a position that the kalam principle 'every contingent needs a cause' depends on recognizing contingency as temporal origination, not on possibility in itself.

Correction: This passage clarifies that only when possibility is tied to temporal origination does the intellect see the need.

Key Takeaway
The intellect does not judge that something needs a cause based solely on its possibility; temporal origination must be present or imagined for the judgment to occur.

Second Objection: Two Evidences Against the Need for a Preponderator
Presents two arguments against the universal necessity of a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two historical and logical arguments that challenge the self-evident necessity of a cause for every contingent event.

Core Idea
Rational people do not universally affirm that the contingent needs a preponderator.

This passage begins the second major objection to a foundational principle of kalam: that every possible thing requires a preponderator (murajjih). The author presents two evidences—the first from theological consensus, the second from everyday human choice—to show that the principle is not self-evident. By examining these counterexamples, we will see why the necessity of a cause was debated among rational thinkers.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's aim
The objection targets the claim that all rational people are certain the possible needs a cause. Read the opening sentence to grasp the challenge.
2
Examine the first evidence
The first evidence points to the majority who affirm the world's temporal origination (huduth) yet believe God's agency began without a prior cause. If this were impossible by intuition, such agreement would not occur.
Notice the appeal to consensus (itbaq) as a proof against self-evidence.
3
Examine the second evidence
The second evidence gives everyday examples: a person choosing between two equal paths or two equal cups of water without a reason. These are widely accepted as possible, contradicting the claim that preference always needs a cause.
These examples are meant to show that tarjih (preference) can occur without murajjih.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
The author concludes that if the premise were self-evidently false, so many rational people would not accept it. This challenges the foundational principle of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني على أصل الكلام : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن العقلاء يجزمون بافتقار الممكن إلى المرجح ، والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن القائلين بحدوث العالم أكثر عدداً من القائلين بقدمه ، ثم إنهم مع كثرتهم يلتزمون أنه تعالى صار فاعلاً للعالم بعد أن لم يكن فاعلاً له ثم اتفقوا على أن تجدد هذه الفاعلية ليس بسبب ، فهؤلاء قد اتفقوا على حصول معنى الحدوث والتجدد في هذه الصورة لا لسبب ، ولو كان امتناع ذلك معلوما بالضرورة لامتنع إطباق العقلاء عليه . الثاني : إنهم يقولون : الهارب من السبع إذا عن له طريقان متساويان من كل الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني ، لا لمرجح ، وكذلك من خير بين الشرب من قدحين من الماء متساويين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الثاني لا لمرجح ، وفي أمثلة هذا الباب كثرة مشهورة. فههنا اتفق الأكثرون على أنه يحصل الرجحان لا المرجح ، ولو كانت هذه المقدمة معلومة الامتناع بالبديهية لما ذهب إلى القول بصحتها طائفة عظيمة من العقلاء
The second objection to the foundation of kalam: we say: we do not accept that rational people are certain that the possible is in need of the preponderator (al-murajjih). What indicates that are points: First: Those who affirm the temporal origination of the world are more numerous than those who affirm its eternity. Then they, despite their number, maintain that He, exalted, became an agent for the world after not having been an agent for it, and they agreed that this renewal (tajaddud) of agency is not due to a cause. So these have agreed that the meaning of temporal origination and renewal occurs in this case without a cause. And if its impossibility were known by necessity, then the agreement of rational people upon it would be impossible. Second: They say: the one fleeing from a wild animal when two equal paths present themselves in every respect, he chooses one of them without a preponderator. Likewise, one who is given a choice between drinking from two equal cups of water, he chooses one without a preponderator. And there are many well-known examples of this type. So here the majority have agreed that preference (tarjih) occurs without a preponderator. And if this premise were known to be impossible by intuition, then a great group of rational people would not have gone to affirm its validity.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage begins the second objection against the foundational kalam principle that every possible needs a preponderator (murajjih). It offers two evidences. The first evidence points to the majority of rational people who affirm the temporal origination of the world (huduth al-‘alam) and at the same time hold that God's creative act began after not acting, and they do not posit a cause for this new agency. If the impossibility of a new act without a cause were self-evident (daruri), then such unanimous agreement (itbaq) would not occur. The second evidence cites everyday examples such as a person choosing between two equal paths or two equal cups of water without any distinguishing factor, which people accept as possible. The author argues that the widespread acceptance of such scenarios by rational people shows that the necessity of a cause is not a self-evident truth.

Key Terms

الحدوث
القدم
التجدد
الرجحان
البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a cause for every contingent event is a self-evident truth universally acknowledged by rational people.
Correction: The passage shows that many rational people, including a majority of theologians and ordinary individuals in everyday decisions, do not affirm this principle universally, indicating it is not self-evident.

Checkpoint

A) The example of two equal paths
B) The consensus of those who affirm the world's permanence
C) The majority of those who affirm the world's temporal origination also accept that God became an agent without a cause
D) The example of two equal cups of water

Key Takeaway
The necessity of a cause for every contingent event is not a self-evident truth, as many rational people accept scenarios where preference occurs without a preponderator.

The Equality of Essences Argument: Distinction Without a Cause
Third evidence: argument from equality of essences leading to uncaused distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological argument that essences are equal and therefore their distinguishing features must occur without a cause, challenging the self-evidence of the need for a preponderator.

Core Idea
If every distinction between essences required a cause, an infinite regress would follow.

This passage presents a third argument from a group of theologians who claim that essences (dhawat) are inherently equal. They provide two proofs for this equality. Then they argue that since essences are equal, any distinction between them must arise without a cause—otherwise we fall into an infinite regress or circular reasoning. This directly challenges the assumption that every possible thing needs a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of equality
Read carefully the opening claim that a group of theologians hold that essences are equal in being essences. They provide two proofs to support this. Focus on grasping why equality is important for the argument.
2
Analyze the two proofs
The first proof uses division: essence can be divided into necessary, possible, abstract, material, so 'essence' is common. The second proof uses conceptualization: we can conceive an essence and later attribute necessity or possibility without altering its essence-ness. Notice how each proof establishes essence as a neutral common factor.
The two proofs are complementary.
3
Follow the main argument
Once equality is established, the argument proceeds: each essence has a distinguishing feature (mumayyiz). If that feature were caused by another matter, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, the distinction must occur without any cause or preponderator. Trace this logical chain carefully.
Pay special attention to the terms dawr and tasalsul.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that some possible things occur without a preponderator, which invalidates the claim that the need for a preponderator is self-evident (badiha). Consider how this challenges the first principle of causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين ذهبوا إلى أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات قالوا ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن الذات يمكن تقسيمها إلى الواجب والممكن والمجرد والمادي فمورد التقسيم مشترك بين الأقسام . الثاني : إنا إذا عقلنا الذوات . فإن عقلنا بعد ذلك : كونه واجباً لذاته ، بقي المعتقد الأول . وإن اعتقدنا كونه ممكنا لذاته زال اعتقاد كونه واجباً لذاته ، ولكن لا يزول اعتقاد كونه ذاتاً فثبت أن كونه ذاتاً قدر مشترك بين الواجب والممكن والمادي والمجرد ، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا شك أن ذات واجب الوجود ممتازة عن سائر الذوات بقيد من القيود ، وكذلك ذات العقل ممتازة عن ذات الجسم بقيد من القيود إذ لو لم يحصل أمر من الأمور ، به يحصل الامتياز ، لما حصل الامتياز البتة ، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ثبت أن الذوات من حيث إنها ذوات أمور متساوية ، ثم إن كل واحد منها اختص بأمر لأجله امتاز عن غيره ، فاختصاص كل واحد منها بذلك المميز ، إن كان لأمر آخر لزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل وهما محالان ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص حاصلاً ، لا لأمر ولا لمرجح ، فثبت بهذا الدليل : وقوع الممكن لا عن مرجح ، وأيضا : فهو قول قال به طائفة عظيمة من العقلاء . وكل ذلك يبطل القول بأن افتقار الممكن إلى المرجح مقدمة معلومة بالبديهة
Third: Indeed a great group of theologians (mutakallimun) held that essences (dhawat) are equal in being essences. They said, and it is indicated by two aspects: First: The essence can be divided into the necessary (al-wajib), the possible (al-mumkin), the abstract (al-mujarrad), and the material (al-madi). So the subject of division is common to the divisions. Second: When we conceive essences, then if we conceive afterwards its being necessary by itself, the first belief remains. And if we believe it possible by itself, the belief that it is necessary by itself departs, but the belief that it is an essence does not depart. So it is established that its being an essence is a common factor between the necessary, the possible, the material, and the abstract. Once this is established, we say: there is no doubt that the essence of the Necessary of Existence is distinguished from other essences by some restriction (qayd). Likewise, the essence of the intellect is distinguished from the essence of the body by some restriction. For if no matter occurred by which distinction occurs, then distinction would not occur at all. Once this is established, we say: it is established that essences insofar as they are essences are equal things. Then each one of them is specified by a matter on account of which it is distinguished from others. So the specification of each one of them by that distinguisher, if it were for another matter, then either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, both of which are impossible. So it is necessary that that specification occurs not due to a matter nor due to a preponderator. Thus it is established by this proof: the occurrence of the possible without a preponderator. And also: it is a statement held by a great group of rational people. And all of this invalidates the statement that the need of the possible for a preponderator is a premise known by intuition (badiha).
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the third evidence of the second objection. It cites a group of theologians who argue that essences (dhawat) are equal qua essences. They provide two proofs. First, essence can be divided into various categories (necessary, possible, abstract, material), showing that 'essence' is a common predicate. Second, when we conceptualize an essence, we can later attribute necessity or possibility without changing its essence-ness, proving that essence itself is neutral. Then the argument proceeds: since essences are equal, each must have some distinguishing feature (mumayyiz) that sets it apart. If that distinguishing feature were due to another cause, there would be an infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, the distinguishing feature must occur without any cause or preponderator. Hence, it is possible for something to occur without a preponderator, contradicting the claim that the need for a preponderator is self-evident.

الواجب
الممكن
المجرد
المادي
القيد
المميز
الدور
التسلسل
البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All distinctions between essences must have a cause.
Correction: This argument shows that if every distinction required a cause, an infinite regress would result; therefore, at least one distinction must be uncaused.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from equality of essences, what follows if the distinguishing feature (mumayyiz) of an essence were due to another cause?
A) It would confirm the need for a preponderator
B) It would lead to circularity or infinite regress
C) It would be impossible for essences to be equal
D) It would establish that essences are not equal

Key Takeaway
The equality of essences argument forces the conclusion that some distinctions occur without any cause, thereby challenging the foundational claim that every possible thing requires a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to trust Allah even when you see no cause for your trials or blessings?

Have you ever excused yourself from deeper reflection by saying 'it's just obvious'? What would it cost you to question what you think is obvious and build your certainty on solid proof?

Third Objection: The Need for Pre-Existing Matter
Analogy between consensus for agent and matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection uses rational consensus to argue for the need for pre-existing matter in creation, and why this challenges the theologians' position.

Core Idea
Consensus for a builder also implies consensus for pre-existing matter.

This passage presents the third objection from those who argue that rational consensus is not limited to the need for an agent. Just as people agree a building needs a builder, they also agree it needs materials. The theologians accept the first but reject the second, leading to an inconsistency. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Read the passage and identify the two consensuses: the need for a builder and the need for pre-existing matter. Notice how the objection draws a parallel between them.
2
Understand the inconsistency
The objection claims that if consensus about the builder is accepted as proof, then consensus about matter should also be accepted. The theologians' rejection of the latter while accepting the former is inconsistent.
The text is cut off, but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : إنا كما رأينا أن أكثر العقلاء أطبقوا على أن الحادث لا بد له من محدث ، والبناء لا بد له من باني ، فكذلك نراهم مطبقين على مقدمات أخرى ، مع أن المتكلمين يزعمون أنها غير صحيحة . فالأول : إنهم كما استبعدوا حدوث البناء من غير باني ، فكذلك استبعدوا حدوث البناء من غير مادة سابقة ، مثل أن يحدث البناء من غير سبق تراب ولا حجر ولا خشب ، فلو صار جزم العقلاء بافتقار البناء إلى الفاعل حجة في صحة هذه المقدمة ، وجب أن يكون جزمهم بافتقار البناء إلى مادة سابقة حجة في صحة هذه المقدمة ، إلا أن المتكلمين يزعمون أنه لا يحتاج حدوث الشيء إلى مادة سابقة وذلك يدل على أن إطباق جمهور العقلاء بحكم
The third objection: We, just as we have seen that most rational people have agreed that the temporally originated (al-hadith) necessarily needs a cause of origination (muhdith), and the building necessarily needs a builder (bāni), similarly we see them agreeing upon other premises, while the theologians claim that they are not valid. So the first: Just as they considered the occurrence of a building without a builder improbable, likewise they considered the occurrence of a building without pre-existing matter improbable, such as the occurrence of a building without previously existing earth, stone, or wood. So if the certainty of rational people that the building needs an agent is a proof for the validity of this premise, then it must be that their certainty that the building needs pre-existing matter is a proof for the validity of this premise. However, the theologians claim that the occurrence of a thing does not need pre-existing matter, and that indicates that the consensus of the multitude of rational people...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage begins the third objection. It draws an analogy: rational people agree that a building needs a builder (a cause) and also agree that a building needs pre-existing materials (matter). The theologians (mutakallimun) accept the first consensus as proof that everything originated needs an originator, but they deny the need for pre-existing matter, claiming that God creates ex nihilo. The objection argues that if consensus is used to prove the need for an agent, it should also prove the need for pre-existing matter. The text is cut off but the implication is that the theologians' selective use of consensus is inconsistent.

Objection and answer on the need for pre-existing material
Sanusi presents and refutes an objection equating the need for a builder with the need for pre-existing material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the theologians' position against the objection that rational consensus equally demands pre-existing material for creation, and the principle of distinguishing original innate disposition from conditioned opinion.

Core Idea
Consensus alone cannot dictate metaphysical necessity; only the untrained innate disposition is the true criterion.

This lesson presents a sophisticated objection against the theologians' reliance on rational consensus for the need of a creator. The opponent argues that the same consensus would also require pre-existing material, which the theologians deny. Sanusi's reply carefully distinguishes between the original innate disposition (fiṭrah) and opinions distorted by debate, showing that the theologians are not inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The opponent compares the need for a builder to the need for pre-existing material. Understand why this analogy threatens the theologians' position.
3
Follow the author's distinction
The author introduces the concept of original innate disposition (fiṭrah aṣliyyah) as the criterion. See how he argues that both propositions are equally supported by fiṭrah when free from disputation.
This is the key to the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : إنا كما رأينا أن أكثر العقلاء أطبقوا على أن الحادث لا بد له من محدث ، والبناء لا بد له من باني ، فكذلك نراهم مطبقين على مقدمات أخرى ، مع أن المتكلمين يزعمون أنها [ غير ] صحيحة . فالأول : إنهم كما استبعدوا حدوث البناء من غير باني ، فكذلك استبعدوا حدوث البناء من غير مادة سابقة ، مثل أن يحدث البناء من غير سبق تراب ولا حجر ولا خشب ، فلو صار جزم العقلاء بافتقار البناء إلى الفاعل حجة [ في صحة هذه المقدمة ، وجب أن يكون ] جزمهم بافتقار البناء إلى مادة سابقة حجة في صحة هذه المقدمة ، إلا أن المتكلمين يزعمون أنه لا يحتاج حدوث الشيء إلى مادة سابقة وذلك يدل على أن إطباق جمهور العقلاء بحكم الفطرة الأصلية لا يدل على كون ذلك الحكم صحيحاً . فإن قالوا إنهم مطبقون بحكم الفطرة الأصلية على افتقار البناء إلى الباني ، وليسوا مطبقين على افتقار البناء إلى المادة السابقة بدليل أن المتكلمين يخالفونهم في صحة هذه المقدمة فنقول في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : إنا إذا أردنا تمييز القضايا الفطرية الأصلية عن غيرها ، فالمعتبر في ذلك التمييز حكم الفطر الأصلية التي لم تتعود المشاغبات والمجادلات ولم تألف التزام المكابرات ، ومن المعلوم أن حكم جمهور الخلق في إحدى القضيتين مثل حكمهم في القضية الأخرى . بقي أن يقال : إن افتقار البناء إلى المادة السابقة يخالف فيه طائفة من المتكلمين . فيقال : وافتقار البناء إلى الباني يخالف فيه قوم آخرون ، فإن كان ظهور المخالفة من الجمع القليل قادحاً في كون القضية بديهية ، فهذا المعنى حاصل في الطرفين، وإن كان لا عبرة بموافقة أهل الجدل والشغب وبمخالفتهم ، وإنما العبرة بحكم الفطرة الأصلية والنفوس الخالية عن النقوش الباطلة ، فهذا المعنى حاصل في البابين فظهر أنه لا سبيل إلى الفرق .
Third Question: Just as we have seen that most rational people have agreed that the originated event necessarily has an originator, and a building necessarily has a builder, so also we see them agreeing on other premises, even though the theologians claim that they are not sound. First: Just as they considered the occurrence of a building without a builder improbable, so also they considered the occurrence of a building without pre-existing material improbable, such as the building occurring without preceding dust, stone, or wood. For if the certainty of rational people that the building needs an agent became a proof for the soundness of this premise, then their certainty that the building needs pre-existing material would have to be a proof for the soundness of this premise. However, the theologians claim that the origination of a thing does not need pre-existing material, and that indicates that the consensus of the majority of rational people based on original innate disposition does not indicate that judgment is sound. If they say that they agree based on original innate disposition that the building needs the builder, but they do not agree that the building needs pre-existing material, evidence being that the theologians oppose them on the soundness of this premise, we say in answer to this question: When we want to distinguish original innate judgments from others, the criterion for that distinction is the judgment of original innate dispositions that have not become accustomed to disputations and debates, nor accustomed to insisting on obstinacies. It is known that the judgment of the majority of people about one of the two propositions is like their judgment about the other proposition. It remains to be said that a group of theologians oppose the need of the building for pre-existing material. It is said: And the need of the building for the builder is opposed by other people. If the appearance of opposition from a small group is detrimental to the proposition being self-evident, then this meaning is present in both sides. And if there is no consideration given to the agreement or opposition of people of debate and contention, but rather consideration is given to the judgment of original innate disposition and souls free from false imprints, then this meaning is present in both cases. So it appears that there is no way to differentiate.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection claims that just as rational people agree that a building needs a builder, they also agree that it needs pre-existing material; therefore, the theologians' rejection of the need for pre-existing material is inconsistent if they rely on consensus for the need for a builder. The answer distinguishes between the innate disposition (fiṭrah) of the common people and the corruption introduced by dialectical habits. The author argues that both propositions equally enjoy innate consensus if we consider the untrained innate disposition. The fact that some theologians oppose one does not invalidate it, because the other is also opposed by some groups. Hence, no distinction can be made between the two cases based on consensus.

Key Terms
حادث (ḥādith, originated event)
محدث (muḥdith, originator)
فطرة أصلية (fiṭrah aṣliyyah, original innate disposition)
بديهة (badīhah, self-evidence)
مادة سابقة (māddah sābiqah, pre-existing material)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the author denies the need for pre-existing material absolutely.
Correction: He merely argues that consensus is not a valid criterion to establish it as a necessity; the need for material may still be true on other grounds.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that rational people agree that a building needs a builder and also pre-existing material?
A) He denies that rational people actually agree on the need for pre-existing material.
B) He argues that the innate disposition of the common people supports both equally, and opposition from some specialists does not affect it.
C) He accepts the objection and admits that the theologians are inconsistent.
D) He asserts that the need for a builder is self-evident while the need for material is not.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that rational consensus alone is not enough to establish metaphysical necessity; one must distinguish between the original innate disposition and opinions corrupted by disputation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accepted a popular belief without checking it against your original innate disposition (fiṭrah) that is free from disputation? What might it cost you to ignore that inner conviction?
?
The author warns that constant debating can corrupt your innate judgment. In what area of your life have you allowed endless arguments to make you doubt something you originally knew was true? Will you return to that original clarity starting today?
?
This passage shows that even widespread agreement can be wrong. What is one belief you hold because 'everyone says so' that you need to re-evaluate based on your pure fiṭrah? What step will you take to uncover the truth?
