The Fifteenth Shubha: Impossibility of Causal Influence
Sanusi presents the fifteenth sophistical objection against causality as a dilemma with two branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and reasoning of the fifteenth sophistical objection against causal influence, and why it claims that the very concept of causation leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
Causal influence is impossible if every cause must either be a cause by being a cause or not.

This lesson unpacks a sophisticated argument against causation. The objection, known as the fifteenth shubha, presents a dilemma: if one thing influences another, the influence must be either by virtue of the effecter being an effecter or not. Both options are shown to lead to absurdities. By working through the argument, you will see how the kalam tradition grapples with causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the formal structure: conditional statement, division into two, then refutation of each.
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage sets up a dilemma: if influence occurs, it is either by virtue of the effecter's being an effecter or not. Write down these two options.
3
Examine the first branch
The first option leads to circular priority: the effecterness is a relation posterior to the effect's essence, but the effect's essence depends on it, creating a priority contradiction. Follow the reasoning step by step.
Key terms: 'متأخر بالرتبة' (posterior in rank) and 'تقدم' (priority).
4
Examine the second branch
The second option leads to a contradiction: if the effecter influences not by virtue of being an effecter, then it both is and is not an effecter in that influence. See how this is a 'combination of two contradictories'.
The phrase 'الجمع بين النقيضين' is crucial.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Both divisions are deemed impossible, so the argument concludes that causal influence is impossible. Consider how this challenges the commonsense view of causality.
This objection is a sophistical (shubha) that kalam theologians would need to refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة عشر : لو أثر شيء في شيء لكان تأثير المؤثر في الأثر، إما أن يكون باعتبار كونه مؤثراً، أو كان ذلك التأثير حاصلاً باعتبار آخر سوى كونه مؤثراً . والقسمان باطلان، فالقول بالتأثير باطل وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يؤثر فيه باعتبار كونه مؤثراً، وذلك لأن كون الشيء مؤثراً في غيره نسبة مخصوصة بين ذات المؤثر، وذات الأثر، والنسبة الحاصلة بين الشيئين متأخرة بالرتبة عن الذاتين (۱)، فيلزم من هذا أن يقال : إن كون المؤثر مؤثراً في ذات الأثر نسبة متأخرة بالرتبة عن ذات الأثر، فلو حكمنا بأن ذات الأثر إنما بسبب تلك المؤثرية، لزم أن تكون هذه المؤثرية متقدمة بالرتبة على ذات الأثر، وحينئذ يلزم تقدم كل واحد منهما على الآخر، أعني (۳) المؤثرية، وذات الأثر وذلك محال، وأما بيان فساد القسم الثاني، وهو أن يؤشر المؤثر في الأثر لا باعتبار كونه مؤثراً فيه [ فهذا أيضاً باطل، لأنه لو أثر فيه لا باعتبار كونه مؤثراً فيه ] (٤) فحينئذ يصير ذلك لا باعتبار المؤثرية، وحينئذ يلزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال
The fifteenth sophistical objection: If one thing influenced another, then the influence of the effecter on the effect would be either by virtue of its being an effecter, or that influence would occur by virtue of something other than its being an effecter. Both divisions are invalid, so the claim of influence is invalid. We say: It is impossible for it to influence by virtue of its being an effecter, because the thing's being an effecter of another is a specific relation between the essence of the effecter and the essence of the effect, and the relation occurring between two things is posterior in rank to the two essences. From this it follows that the effecter's being an effecter of the essence of the effect is a relation posterior in rank to the essence of the effect. So if we judge that the essence of the effect is due to that effecterness, then this effecterness must be prior in rank to the essence of the effect. Then priority of each over the other would follow — I mean effecterness and the essence of the effect — and that is impossible. As for the demonstration of the invalidity of the second division, which is that the effecter influences the effect not by virtue of its being an effecter, this is also invalid, because if it influences it not by virtue of its being an effecter, then it becomes not by virtue of effecterness, and then the combination of two contradictories would follow, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth sophistical objection against the possibility of causal influence. The argument is structured as a dilemma: if something influences another, the influence must be either in virtue of the effecter's being an effecter or not. Both options are argued to be impossible. For the first option, the author explains that being an effecter is a relation between the effecter and the effect, and a relation is posterior to the essences. But if the effect's essence is caused by that relation (effecterness), then the relation must be prior to the effect's essence, creating a circular priority contradiction. For the second option, if the effecter influences without being an effecter, then it is not influencing as an effecter, which leads to a contradiction (the effecter both influences and does not influence as an effecter). Thus, the very concept of causal influence is deemed impossible. This objection is a sophisticated attack on the causal principle.

Key Terms
تأثير
مؤثر
أثر
نسبة
متأخر بالرتبة
الجمع بين النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fifteenth shubha, what is the first division of causal influence that is considered?
A) Influence by virtue of the effecter's essence.
B) Influence by virtue of the effecter's being an effecter.
C) Influence by virtue of something external.
D) Influence by virtue of the effect's essence.

Key Takeaway
The fifteenth shubha argues that the concept of causal influence leads to impossible circularity or contradiction, challenging its coherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face a setback, do you blame secondary causes or do you first turn to the Creator? The shubha shows that attributing influence to creatures leads to logical contradiction. Does your heart truly accept that nothing acts except God?

The Sixteenth Shubha: Circular Priority in Causation
The sixteenth sophistical objection on circular priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixteenth sophistical objection that attempts to show causation leads to a circular priority between the necessity of an effect and its existence.

Core Idea
Causation requires necessity, but necessity creates an impossible circular priority.

This lesson examines the sixteenth shubha, a sophistical argument against causation. The objector claims that for an effect to exist, it must first become necessary from its cause, yet that necessity is an attribute of existence itself, leading to a contradiction. As you read, trace the logical steps that aim to prove causation is incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side. Identify the core claim: an effect must be necessary from its cause to exist.
2
Identify the key premises
Note the two premises: (1) Without necessity, the effect cannot exist. (2) Necessity is an attribute of existence, so it is posterior to the effect.
These premises lead to the paradox.
3
Understand the circularity argument
See how the objector concludes that the necessity must be both prior (as a condition) and posterior (as an attribute) to the effect's existence, which is impossible.
This is the crux of the objection.
4
Consider why this is sophistical
Reflect on why this might be fallacious. The lesson does not provide the refutation, but think about whether the necessity is truly an attribute of the effect's existence or something else.
This prepares you for the author's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة عشر : الأثر ما لم يجب وجوده عن المؤثر، امتنع دخوله في الوجود، فإن قيل (٥) إن [ مؤثر الأثر واجب الصدور عن المؤثر فيكون ممكن الصدور عنه، وما دام يكون الشيء باقياً على إمكانه ] امتنع دخوله [ في الوجود ]، فيثبت أن الأثر ما لم يصر واجب الصدور عن المؤثر امتنع دخوله في الوجود، وإذا ثبت هذا لزم أن يقال : إن صيرورة واجب الصدور عن المؤثر متقدم بالرتبة على دخوله في الوجود، لكن وجوب صدوره عن المؤثر صفة من صفات وجوده، والصفة متأخرة بالرتبة عن الموصوف، فهذا يقتضي تقدم كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين على الآخر، وتأخره عنه، وذلك محال
The sixteenth sophistical objection: The effect, as long as its existence from the effecter is not necessary, is prevented from entering into existence. If it is said that the effecter of the effect [?] is necessarily emanating from the effecter, then it is possibly emanating from it, and as long as the thing remains upon its possibility, it is prevented from entering into existence. So it is established that the effect, as long as it has not become necessarily emanating from the effecter, is prevented from entering into existence. And when this is established, it must be said: The becoming necessarily emanating from the effecter is prior in rank to its entering into existence. But the necessity of its emanation from the effecter is an attribute of its existence, and the attribute is posterior in rank to the described. Thus this requires the priority of each of these two considerations over the other and its posteriority, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the sixteenth sophistical objection. It argues that for an effect to exist, it must be necessary from its cause (i.e., its existence must be necessitated). Otherwise, it remains merely possible and cannot enter existence. But then the necessity of emanation is a necessary condition before existence, so it must be prior to existence. However, that necessity is an attribute of the effect's existence (since it is the necessity of something's existence from the cause), and attributes are posterior to the described essence. Therefore, this requirement forces a circular priority: the necessity must be both prior and posterior to the effect's existence, which is impossible. Thus, the concept of causation is again shown to be incoherent.

Key Terms
أثر (effect)
وجوب (necessity)
امكان (possibility)
صفة (attribute)
متقدم بالرتبة (prior in rank)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the necessity of the effect from the cause is a condition that must be prior, and that it is an attribute posterior, causing a genuine paradox.
Correction: The objection assumes that necessity is an attribute of the effect's existence, but it may instead be a relation or a condition that is not an attribute in the same sense. The author will later refute this.

Checkpoint
According to the sixteenth shubha, what must happen before an effect can enter existence?
A) The effect must become necessary from the effecter.
B) The effect must become possible.
C) The effect must become an attribute.
D) The effect must become prior to the cause.

Key Takeaway
The sixteenth shubha attempts to disprove causation by arguing that the necessity of the effect creates an impossible circular priority, but this depends on assuming necessity is an attribute of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the chain of events in your life, do you ever assume that one thing must happen because of another? How does this shubha expose a hidden assumption in your own thinking about cause and effect?
?
The objector treats necessity as an attribute of the effect's existence. Reflect on your own view of Allah's attributes: have you ever confused a relational quality with an inherent attribute? What does this call to be more precise in your understanding?
?
This objection suggests that human reasoning about causation leads to contradiction. In your daily reliance on causes (e.g., for your provision, health, or salvation), does this shake your trust? How can you affirm causality as a sign of Allah's power without falling into this sophism?

Incomplete opening of the seventeenth shubha

Core Idea
An incomplete argument cannot be judged.

This passage presents only the opening line of the seventeenth objection against causality. The text cuts off, leaving the argument unfinished. This incompleteness is itself a lesson: in kalam, every objection must be fully stated before it can be refuted.

Guided Path

Reflect on why an incomplete argument cannot be assessed or refuted. In kalam debates, every objection must be fully articulated for a proper response.
This passage serves as a reminder of the need for complete sources.

Shubha 17: Why Possibility and Need Cannot Be the Cause of Influence
Presents the seventeenth argument that possibility and need cannot be causes of causal influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the seventeenth argument against causal influence fails by exposing the absurd consequences of attributing causality to non-existent attributes like possibility and need.

Core Idea
Possibility and need are non-existent; they cannot be causes of existence or influence.

This lesson examines the seventeenth skeptical argument concerning causation. The argument attempts to show that the very prerequisites of being influenced—possibility and need—would themselves have to be causes of the cause's influence, leading to two absurdities. We will see how the response exposes the impossibility of non-existent things acting as causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: the argument states a condition and then draws a consequence.
The argument is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the two absurdities
The passage names two reasons why the consequence is impossible. Find them and understand each: (1) something contingent influences the Necessary, (2) non-existence causes existence.
These are the core refutation.
3
Reflect on the nature of possibility and need
The argument emphasizes that possibility and need are non-existent attributes. Why does that matter for causation? Consider how this connects to the second absurdity.
This is a key theological insight.
4
Consider the broader implication
Ask yourself: How does this argument fit into the larger discussion of causality in Islamic theology? Why is it important that only existent things can be causes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة عشر : المؤثر يمتنع كونه مؤثراً في الشيء، إلا إذا كان ذلك الشيء في نفسه ممكن الوجود محتاجاً إلى الغير، فثبت أن عند عدم الإمكان والحاجة يمتنع كون الشيء مؤثراً فيه، فلو صار مؤثراً فيه عند حصول الإمكان والحاجة لزم أن يكون هذا الإمكان وهذه الحاجة علة لكون ذلك المباين علة لوجوده، وهذا محال لوجهين: الأول: إنه يلزم أن يحصل لغير واجب الوجود لذاته تأثير في ذات واجب الوجود لذاته وهو محال. الثاني: إنا قد دللنا على أن هذا الإمكان وهذه الحاجة أمران عدميان، ولو جعلناهما علة لكون العلة الفاعلة مؤثرة في وجود الأثر لكانت العلة الأولى لهذه الموجودات أمراً عدمياً، وحينئذ يلزم كون العدم علة الوجود وهذا محال.
The seventeenth suspicious argument: The cause is impossible to be an influencer in a thing unless that thing is in itself possible of existence, needing the other. So it is established that in the absence of possibility and need, it is impossible for the thing to be influenced. So if it becomes influenced when possibility and need occur, then this possibility and this need would be the cause of that [thing] being a cause for its existence. And this is impossible for two reasons: First, it entails that something other than the Necessarily Existent in Itself obtains influence in the essence of the Necessarily Existent in Itself, and that is impossible. Second, we have proven that this possibility and this need are two non-existent things, and if we make them a cause for the active cause being influential in the existence of the effect, then the first cause for these existents would be a non-existent thing, and then it would entail that non-existence is the cause of existence, and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the seventeenth argument. It claims that for a cause to be an influencer in a thing, that thing must be possible and in need. So possibility and need are prerequisites. If the effect becomes influenced only when these prerequisites are present, then these prerequisites become the cause for the cause's influence. This leads to two absurdities: First, it would mean that something contingent (the possibility and need) has an effect on the Necessary Being (the cause), which is impossible. Second, since possibility and need are non-existent (they are privations), it would entail that non-existence is a cause of existence, which is also impossible.

Key Terms
ممكن (mumkin)
محتاج (muḥtāj)
عدم ('adam)
علة ('illa)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think possibility and need are positive attributes that can have causal efficacy.
Correction: The argument clarifies that possibility and need are non-existent (privations), not positive realities, and thus cannot be causes of anything.

Checkpoint
What two absurdities does the seventeenth argument identify if possibility and need were causes of the cause's influence?
A) Influence of contingent on Necessary and priority of existence over non-existence.
B) Influence of contingent on Necessary and non-existence as cause of existence.
C) Circularity and infinite regress.
D) Dependence of the cause on the effect and vice versa.

Key Takeaway
The seventeenth argument fails because it would entail either a contingent influencing the Necessary or non-existence causing existence, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument shows that even the most basic prerequisites for causation cannot be attributed to contingent things. Has your heart ever depended on something contingent—like status, wealth, or relationships—as if it were a real cause? What would it mean for you to truly recognize that only the Necessary Being is the real cause?

The Dilemma of Pre-existence of Influence
Presents the eighteenth argument against causal influence, leading to circularity, regress, or independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the trilemma that the opponent of causal influence raises: if a cause becomes an influencer only when the effect exists, then the influence itself requires a cause, leading to circularity, infinite regress, or independence of the effect.

Core Idea
A cause's influence cannot arise without a cause of its own.

This final argument against real causal influence focuses on the moment when the cause becomes an actual influencer. The opponent argues that the influence itself is a new event that must have a cause, but every option leads to absurdity. Let's examine this trilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow sink in.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find the three possibilities presented after the influence occurs: caused by the effect, caused by something else, or uncaused.
Each leads to a different absurdity.
3
Understand why each is problematic
Circularity (dawr), infinite regress (tasalsul), or independence from a cause. The opponent argues all are unacceptable.
This is a classic trilemma argument.
4
See the conclusion
The argument concludes that the concept of real causal influence is incoherent, thus denying real influence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة عشر : إن المؤثر ما كان مؤثراً بالفعل قبل دخول الأثر، فإذا صار مؤثراً بالفعل عند وجود الأثر، فقد حدثت تلك المؤثرية، فإن كان حدوثها لأجل هذا الأثر لزم الدور، وإن كان حدوثها لشيء آخر لزم التسلسل، وإن حدثت هذه المؤثرية لا لسبب أصلاً، كان ذلك قولاً بأن المحدث الممكن غني عن المؤثر والفاعل، فهذا تمام شبهات القائلين بنفي التأثير والأثر.
The eighteenth suspicious argument: The cause is not an influencer in actuality before the entry of the effect. So when it becomes an influencer in actuality at the existence of the effect, then that influence has occurred. If its occurrence is due to this effect, then circularity (al-dawr) is entailed. If its occurrence is due to something else, then infinite regress (al-tasalsul) is entailed. If this influence occurs without any cause at all, then that is a statement that the possible originated thing is independent of the cause and the agent. So this completes the suspicious arguments of those who deny influence and effect.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the eighteenth and final argument in this series. It states that a cause is not actually influencing before the effect exists. So when the effect comes into existence, the cause becomes an influencer (its influence occurs simultaneously). This newly occurring influence must have a cause. Three possibilities are examined: 1) If the influence is caused by the effect itself, that is circular (al-dawr), because the effect would cause its own cause's influence. 2) If it is caused by something else, an infinite regress (al-tasalsul) follows, as each new cause would require its own influence. 3) If it has no cause, then the effect needs no cause, contradicting the assumption that the effect depends on a cause. Thus, all options are absurd, so the very notion of real causal influence is rejected.

Key Terms
تأثير (ta'thīr)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
فاعل (fā'il)
محدث (muḥdath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument only deals with temporal priority.
Correction: It actually deals with the causal priority of the influence itself. The issue is not when the influence happens but what causes the influence to occur.

Checkpoint
In the eighteenth argument, what does it entail if the cause's influence occurs without any cause?
A) Infinite regress.
B) Circularity.
C) The effect is independent of a cause.
D) The cause is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the very concept of causal influence leads to an absurd trilemma, thus denying real influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces us to acknowledge that every influence we have is itself an event that requires a cause. In your daily interactions, where do you take your influence for granted as if it originates from you? How will you consciously remember that your ability to influence is a gift from Allah, and how should that change your humility?
?
The trilemma shows that denying dependency leads to absurdity. Consider a relationship where you have influence (e.g., parent, teacher, friend). Are there moments you act as if your influence is self-sufficient, uncaused? Repent today for any such feeling of independence, and commit to acknowledging Allah as the sole source of all influence.
?
If you truly believed that your actions have real influence only by Allah's permission, how would you approach your next decision that affects others? Write a specific prayer asking Allah to make you a means of good while recognizing your utter dependence on Him.

Answer to the First Objection: Acquisition of the Acquired
The author states the first objection and provides its answer regarding the need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against causal necessity for enduring things and its resolution through distinguishing between two interpretations of 'acquisition of the acquired'.

Core Idea
The objection of 'acquiring the acquired' misunderstands the relation between cause and enduring effect.

One of the first challenges to the principle of causality is the objection that if possibility requires a cause, then something that continues to exist would also need a cause, leading to the absurdity of 'acquiring the acquired'. In this passage, the author clarifies the objection and provides a precise answer, showing that the alleged absurdity does not arise. By distinguishing between a new acquisition and the same existence, the answer preserves the need for a sustaining cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated, then the author responds.
The objection is short; pay attention to the reasoning.
2
Understand 'acquisition of the acquired'
The objection claims that requiring a cause for continued existence leads to tahsil al-hasil, which means trying to obtain what is already obtained. This is considered impossible.
This concept is key to the objection.
3
Identify the two interpretations
The author presents two possible interpretations of what the opponent might mean. Distinguish between them: one leads to a new acquisition, the other to the same existence.
The first interpretation is not necessary; the second is correct.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the enduring thing still needs a cause, because the cause sustains the same existence without requiring a second acquisition. So the objection fails.
This preserves the principle of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : وهي قولهم : ( لو كان الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر ، لكان الباقي في حال بقائه محتاجاً ، إلى المؤثر : .
نقول في الجواب عنها : لانزاع في المقدمة الأولى : لكن لم قلتم : إن الباقي غير مفتقر إلى المؤثر ؟ قوله : ( لأنه يلزم منه تحصيل الحاصل وهو محال ، قلنا : إن عنيتم بهذا الكلام ) أنه يلزم أن يحصل للحاصل حصول آخر ، فهذا غير لازم ، لأن على هذا التقدير يكون هذا الحصول الثاني حصولاً جديداً ، فيكون تأثير المؤثر في الحادث لا في الثاني ، وكلا منا فيما إذا كان المؤثر في نفس الثاني ، وإن عنيتم بهذا الكلام) (۱) أن يكون عين (۲) ذلك الحصول واقعاً بتأثير ذلك المؤثر ، وتكوينه من غير توهم أن يحصل لذلك الحاصل حصول ثان ، فهذا حق ، وصدق عندنا ، فلم قلتم : إن الأمر ليس كذلك ؟
The first objection: their statement: 'If possibility (al-imkan) necessitates a cause (al-mu'aththir), then the enduring thing (al-baqi) in its state of endurance would be in need of the cause.' We say in reply to it: There is no dispute about the first premise, but why do you say that the enduring thing is not in need of the cause? His statement: 'Because it would entail the acquisition of the acquired (tahsil al-hasil), which is impossible.' We say: If by this statement you mean that it is necessary that a second acquisition occur for the acquired, then this is not necessary, because on this supposition this second acquisition would be a new acquisition, so the effect of the cause would be on the new event (al-hadith), not on the second (i.e., the original acquired). And if you mean by this statement that that same acquisition takes place by the effect of that cause and its bringing about, without imagining that a second acquisition occurs for that acquired, then this is true and correct with us, so why do you say that it is impossible?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection and its answer concerning the principle of causality. The objection argues that if possibility (i.e., the state of being contingent) requires a cause for existence, then an enduring entity (something that continues in existence) would also need a cause to sustain it, which leads to the absurdity of 'acquiring what is already acquired' (tahsil al-hasil). The author responds by distinguishing two interpretations. First, if the opponent means that a second acquisition of the very same existence is required, that is not necessary; the second acquisition would be a new acquisition, so the cause affects the new state, not the original. Second, if the opponent means that the very same existence is brought about by the cause (without a second existence), that is exactly what the author affirms as correct. The objection thus fails because it either misunderstands the causal relation or concedes the correct view.

Key Terms
إمكان
مؤثر
باقي
تحصيل الحاصل
حصول
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The claim that requiring a cause for continued existence leads to 'acquiring the acquired' overlooks the distinction between a new state and the original state.
Correction: The cause sustains the same existence; there is no need for a second acquisition of the same thing. The objection mistakenly assumes that sustaining requires repetition.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, what is the consequence if the opponent interprets 'acquisition of the acquired' as a second acquisition of the same existence?
A) It is necessary and valid.
B) It is impossible (muhal).
C) It is not necessary because the second acquisition is new.
D) It proves that the enduring thing does not need a cause.

Key Takeaway
The enduring thing's need for a cause does not lead to 'acquiring the acquired' because the cause sustains the same existence, not a new one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your existence depends on God at every moment, or only at the moment you were created? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that even your continued being is sustained by Him?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you instinctively rely on your own past accomplishments or inherent abilities, forgetting that your very ability to act is being constantly given to you? How can you train your heart to recognize this dependence?
?
Reflect on a moment when you felt self-sufficient. Can you see how that feeling might be a form of the first objection's assumption—that once something is acquired, it no longer needs a cause? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself of your continuous need for God?

Answer to the Second Objection: Bringing into Existence the Existing
The author answers the objection that a cause acting on an effect while it exists entails bringing the existing into existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the objection that causal influence on an existing effect leads to the absurdity of bringing the existing into existence.

Core Idea
Causal dependence of continued existence is not bringing the existing into existence.

This passage presents the author's precise answer to a classic objection: if a cause influences an effect while it exists, does that not entail making something exist that already exists? The author clarifies what 'bringing into existence the existing' means, distinguishing a mistaken interpretation from the correct one. This distinction resolves the apparent absurdity and defends the Ash'ari position on causal continuity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the dialogue structure: the opponent's dilemma, the author's choice, the opponent's charge of absurdity, and the author's clarification.
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the opponent's dilemma
The opponent presents a forced choice: the cause's influence occurs either when the effect exists or when it does not exist. Recognize why this dilemma is posed.
The opponent aims to show both options lead to problems.
3
Examine the author's selection and the opponent's objection
The author chooses the first option (influence during existence). The opponent then objects that this implies 'bringing into existence the existing', which is absurd. Note the term 'ijad al-mawjud'.
This objection assumes that causing something to exist when it already exists is contradictory.
4
Understand the author's two-part clarification
The author distinguishes two meanings of 'ijad al-mawjud'. If it means making something exist from two things (existence and non-existence), that is not necessary. But if it means that the existence is due to the cause's influence, that is exactly the correct doctrine. Reflect on why this resolves the objection.
The opponent mistakes causal dependence for a kind of re-creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم (۳) : « تأثير المؤثر ، إما أن يكون حال وجود الأثر أو حال عدمه ، قلنا : بل حال وجوده . قوله : « فيلزم إيجاد الموجود ، وهو محال ، قلنا : إن عنيت بإيجاد الموجود جعله موجوداً من شيئين (١) ، فهذا غير لازم ، وإن عنيت أن هذا الوجود الحاصل (إنما حصل ) (٢) بتأثير هذا المؤثر فيه ، فهذا مذهبنا الذي لا حق إلا هو ، فلم قلتم إنه محال ؟
And the answer to the second objection: their statement: 'The effect of the cause is either at the time of the existence of the effect or at the time of its non-existence.' We say: Rather at the time of its existence. His statement: 'Then it would necessitate bringing into existence the existing (ijad al-mawjud), which is impossible.' We say: If by 'bringing into existence the existing' you mean making it exist from two things (i.e., from existence and non-existence?), then this is not necessary. And if you mean that this obtained existence only came about by the effect of this cause upon it, then this is our doctrine, than which there is no truth. So why do you say it is impossible?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection. The opponent proposes a dilemma: the cause's influence either occurs while the effect is existing or while it is non-existing. The author chooses the first option (while it exists). The opponent then argues that this would imply 'bringing into existence the existing' (ijad al-mawjud), which is absurd. The author responds by clarifying the meaning. If 'bringing into existence the existing' means making it exist from two things (perhaps combining existence and non-existence?), that is not necessary. But if it means that this existence is due to the cause's influence, that is exactly the correct position (madhhab). Thus the objection is based on a misunderstanding.

Key Terms
تأثير المؤثر
أثر
إيجاد الموجود
مذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causal influence during the effect's existence implies making something exist that already exists, which is impossible.
Correction: The author clarifies that causal influence is about the dependence of continued existence on the cause, not a process of bringing into existence from non-existence. The continued existence is brought about by the cause, which is not absurd.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the second objection, what does the author affirm as his doctrine?
A) The cause acts only at the time of the effect's non-existence.
B) Bringing the existing into existence from two things.
C) The existence of the effect is due to the cause's influence.
D) The effect does not need a cause after it exists.

Key Takeaway
The author defends that causal influence during existence means the effect's existence depends on the cause, not a self-contradictory making of what already is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your very existence at this moment is sustained by God? What would change if you internalized that without God's continuous act, you would cease to exist?
?
Reflect on a time you attributed a good outcome to your own effort rather than God's sustaining power. How can you train yourself to see God's continuous creation in every moment?
?
If your continued existence depends on God every second, how does that affect your sense of dependence and gratitude? What is one practical step you can take today to remind yourself of this truth throughout the day?

Refuting the Claim that Dependence Must Be Positive
Author refutes objection via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that dependence is a positive attribute by showing that it leads to the absurdity that non-existence would exist.

Core Idea
If dependence is positive, non-existence would have an existent attribute — an absurdity.

This passage presents a clever reductio ad absurdum. The opponent claims that the dependence of an effect on its cause must be a positive attribute because it contradicts independence. The author turns the tables, showing that this reasoning would force us to conclude that non-existence itself exists — an impossibility. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then review the translation. Pay attention to the structure: the opponent's claim, the author's initial response, and the reductio.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent argues that dependence must be either a positive or negative attribute. When the author suggests it could be negative, the opponent retorts that dependence is the contradictory of 'not dependent', which implies it is positive. Understand this move.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The author then shows that if being contradictory is a positive attribute, and non-existence is described as contradictory to existence, then non-existence would have a positive attribute and thus would exist. Trace this logical chain step by step.
This is the heart of the refutation.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Since the consequence is impossible (non-existence cannot exist), the opponent's original assumption must be false. The passage ends by noting that any counterargument is already answered here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : ( و افتقار الأثر إلى المؤثر . إما أن يكون وصفاً ثبوتياً أو عدمياً ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون مفهوماً عدمياً ؟ : د كونه مفتقراً نقيض لقولنا : إنه غير مفتقر ) .
قوله قلنا : هذا المكلام يوجب عليكم (4) كون العدم وجوداً ، وذلك لأن كون العدم مناقضاً للوجود ومقابلاً له وصف وجودي ، بدليل أن كونه غير مناقض له ، وغير مقابل له ، وصف علمي وإذا كان اللامناقض واللامقابل وصفا عدمياً ، وجب أن يكون كونه مناقضاً ومقابلاً وصفا وجودياً ، وإذا كان لهذا الوصف وصفاً وجودياً ) وهو محمول العدم ، لأنه لا نزاع أن العدم مناقض للوجود ) () ومقابل له ، فيلزم أن يكون العدم موصوفا بصفة موجودة
، والموصف بالصفة الموجودة موجود، فيلزم كون العدم موجوداً ، وهو محال . وكل ما ذكروه في الجواب عن هذا الكلام ، فهو عين جوابنا عن هذه الشبهة المذكورة .
And the answer to the third objection: their statement: 'The dependence (iftiqar) of the effect on the cause is either a positive attribute (wasf thubuti) or a negative attribute (wasf 'adami).' We say: Why is it not permissible that it be a negative concept? His statement: 'Because being dependent is the contradictory of our statement that it is not dependent.' We say: This statement of yours necessitates that non-existence (al-'adam) is existence (wujud). That is because non-existence's being contradictory to existence and opposite to it is a positive attribute, for proof that its being not contradictory to it and not opposite to it is a negative attribute. And when 'not-contradictory' and 'not-opposite' are negative attributes, it follows that its being contradictory and opposite is a positive attribute. And if this attribute is a positive attribute, and it is predicated of non-existence because there is no dispute that non-existence is contradictory and opposite to existence, then it follows that non-existence is qualified by an existent attribute. And what is qualified by an existent attribute is existent, so it follows that non-existence is existent, which is impossible. And everything they mentioned in replying to this statement is exactly our answer to this mentioned objection.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the third objection. The opponent presents a classification: the dependence of an effect on its cause must be either a positive attribute or a negative attribute. The author first suggests that it could be negative (i.e., a privation). The opponent retorts that dependence is the contradictory of 'not dependent,' which implies it is positive. The author then shows that this reasoning leads to an absurdity: if 'being contradictory to existence' is a positive attribute, and non-existence (al-'adam) is described as contradictory to existence, then non-existence would have a positive attribute and thus would exist—an impossibility. Therefore, the opponent's argument fails. The author concludes that any attempt to answer this is already included in his reply.

Key Terms
افتقار
وصف ثبوتي
وصف عدمي
عدم
نقيض
مقابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dependence must be a positive attribute because it is the contradictory of independence.
Correction: The author demonstrates that this reasoning leads to the absurdity that non-existence would exist, so the argument is invalid.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does the author derive if dependence is considered a positive attribute?
A) The effect would not need a cause.
B) Non-existence would be impossible.
C) Non-existence would have a positive attribute and thus exist.
D) Dependence would be a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that dependence is a positive attribute leads to the absurdity that non-existence exists, so the claim is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the absurdity that non-existence would need to exist to have a positive attribute. How does this logical truth strengthen your conviction that only the Necessary Being (Allah) truly exists without dependence? Have you ever felt that your own existence is 'self-made'? How does this passage correct that thought?
?
The opponent assumed dependence must be a positive attribute because it is the opposite of independence. When have you assumed that something about Allah must be a certain way based on human reasoning? Are you willing to accept 'negative' descriptions of Allah (like 'He does not need a cause') even if they feel less satisfying? What does that require from your heart?
?
This passage shows that flawed reasoning about attributes can lead to impossible conclusions. In your daily life, where might you be making similar logical leaps about faith? Consider a belief you hold about Islam that you have not carefully examined. Will you take one step today to test it against revelation and sound reason?
